<p><b>Mycenaean Greece</b> (or <b>Mycenaean civilization</b>) was the last phase of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> (c. 1600–1100 BC). It represents the first advanced civilization in mainland <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, with its palatial states, urban organization, works of art and writing system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup> Among the centers of power that emerged, the most notable were those of <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midea_(Argolid)" title="Midea (Argolid)">Midea</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Boeotia)" title="Orchomenus (Boeotia)">Orchomenos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Greece" title="Central Greece">Central Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iolcos" title="Iolcos">Iolcos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>. The most prominent site was <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argolid" title="Argolid">Argolid</a>, to which the culture of this era owes its name. Mycenaean and Mycenaean-influenced settlements also appeared in <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> on islands in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, on the coast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Mycenaean Greeks introduced several innovations in the fields of engineering, architecture and military infrastructure, while trade over vast areas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> was essential for the Mycenaean economy. Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllabic_script" title="Syllabic script">syllabic script</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>, offers the first written records of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> and their religion already included several deities that can also be found in the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Olympic Pantheon</a>. Mycenaean Greece was dominated by a warrior elite society and consisted of a network of palace states that developed rigid hierarchical, political, social and economic systems. At the head of this society was the king, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wanax" title="Wanax">wanax</a>.</p>
<p>Mycenaean Greece perished with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">collapse of Bronze Age culture</a> in the eastern Mediterranean, to be followed by the so-called <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, a recordless transitional period leading to <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic Greece</a> where significant shifts occurred from palace-centralized to de-centralized forms of socio-economic organization (including the extensive use of iron).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Various theories have been proposed for the end of this civilization, among them the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a> or activities connected to the “<a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>”. Additional theories such as natural disasters and climatic changes have been also suggested. The Mycenaean period became the historical setting of much ancient <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">Greek literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">mythology</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epic_cycle" title="Epic cycle">Trojan Epic Cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<ul>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Chronology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helladic_period" title="Helladic period">Helladic period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2430" data-file-width="3000" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg/220px-Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg/330px-Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg/440px-Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lions-Gate-Mycenae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Lion_Gate" title="Lion Gate">Lion Gate</a>, the main entrance of the citadel of Mycenae, 13th century BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Bronze Age in mainland Greece is generally termed as the "Helladic period" by modern archaeologists, after <i>Hellas</i>, the Greek name for <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. This period is divided into three subperiods: The Early Helladic (EH) period (c. 2900–2000 BC) was a time of prosperity with the use of metals and a growth in technology, economy and social organization. The Middle Helladic (MH) period (ca. 2000–1650 BC) faced a slower pace of development, as well as the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Megaron" title="Megaron">megaron</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Cist" title="Cist">cist</a> graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup> Finally, the Late Helladic (LH) period (c. 1650–1050 BC) roughly coincides with Mycenaean Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Late Helladic period is further divided into LHI and LHII, both of which coincide with the early period of Mycenaean Greece (c. 1650–1425 BC), and LHIII (c. 1425–1050 BC), the period of expansion, decline and collapse of the Mycenaean civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup> The transition period from the Bronze Age to the Iron Age in Greece is known as Sub-Mycenaean (c. 1050–1000 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Names of the Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Achaeans (Homer)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chariot_Pylos.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="289" data-file-width="250" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Chariot_Pylos.jpg/150px-Chariot_Pylos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Chariot_Pylos.jpg/225px-Chariot_Pylos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/Chariot_Pylos.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="258" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg/150px-Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg/225px-Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Tiryns_chariot_fresco.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Two Mycenaean Greek warriors and chariot on a fresco from <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> (about 1350 BC) (left) and Two Mycenaean female charioteers from <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a>, 1200 BC (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Decipherment" title="Decipherment">decipherment</a> of the Mycenaean <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> script, a writing system adapted for the use of the Greek language of the Late Bronze Age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> demonstrated the continuity of Greek speech from the 2nd millennium BC into the 8th century BC when a new script emerged. Moreover, it revealed that the bearers of Mycenaean culture were ethnically connected with the populations that resided in the Greek peninsula after the end of this cultural period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Various collective terms for the inhabitants of Mycenaean Greece were used by Homer in his 8th century BC epic, the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>, in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>. The latter was supposed to have happened in the late 13th – early 12th century BC, when a coalition of small Greek states under the king of Mycenae, besieged the walled city of <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>. Homer used the <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonyms</a> Achaeans, Danaans and Argives, to refer to the besiegers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> These names appear to have passed down from the time they were in use to the time when Homer applied them as collective terms in his <i>Iliad</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> There is an isolated reference to <i>a-ka-wi-ja-de</i> in the Linear B records in <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>, Crete dated to c. 1400 BC, which most probably refers to a Mycenaean (Achaean) state on the Greek mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Egyptian records mention a <i>T(D)-n-j</i> or <i>Danaya</i> (Tanaju) land for the first time in circa 1437 BC, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thutmoses_III" title="Thutmoses III">Thutmoses III</a> (r. 1479–1425 BC). This land is geographically defined in an inscription from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_III" title="Amenhotep III">Amenhotep III</a> (r. circa 1391/1388–1353/1351 BC), where a number of Danaya cities are mentioned, which cover the largest part of southern mainland Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder4647_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder4647-16">[16]</a></sup> Among them, cities such as Mycenae, Nauplion and Thebes, have been identified with certainty. Danaya has been equated with the ethnonym <i>Danaoi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Δαναοί</span>), the name of the mythical dynasty that ruled in the region of Argos, also used as an ethnonym for the Greek people by Homer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder4647_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder4647-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the official records of another Bronze Age empire, that of the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, various references from c. 1400 BC to 1220 BC mention a country named <i>Ahhiyawa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckmanBryceCline4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeckmanBryceCline4-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Recent scholarship, based on textual evidence, new interpretations of the Hittite inscriptions, as well as on recent surveys of archaeological evidence about Mycenaean-Anatolian contacts during this period, concludes that the term <i>Ahhiyawa</i> must have been used in reference to the Mycenaean world (land of the Achaeans), or at least to a part of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryce58_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryce58-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This term may have also had broader connotations in some texts, possibly referring to all regions settled by Mycenaeans or regions under direct Mycenaean political control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckmanBryceCline4_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-BeckmanBryceCline4-18">[18]</a></sup> Another similar ethnonym <i>Ekwesh</i> in 12th century BC Egyptian inscriptions, has been commonly identified with the Ahhiyawans. These Ekwesh were mentioned as a group of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_People" title="Sea People">Sea People</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaft_Grave_era_.28c._1600.E2.80.931450_BC.29">Shaft Grave era (c. 1600–1450 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Shaft Grave era (c. 1600–1450 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1748" data-file-width="1748" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/220px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/330px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/440px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Death mask, known as the <a href="/wiki/Mask_of_Agamemnon" title="Mask of Agamemnon">Mask of Agamemnon</a>, Grave Circle A, Mycenae, 16th century BC, probably the most famous artifact of Mycenaean Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mycenaean civilization originated and evolved from the society and culture of the Early and Middle Helladic period in mainland Greece under influences from <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan Crete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickinson_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson-24">[24]</a></sup> Towards the end of the Middle Bronze Age (c. 1600 BC) a significant increase in the population and the number of settlements occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield31_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield31-25">[25]</a></sup> A number of centers of power emerged in southern mainland Greece dominated by a warrior elite society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickinson_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson-24">[24]</a></sup> while the typical dwellings of that era were an early type of <a href="/wiki/Megaron" title="Megaron">megaron</a> buildings. Some more complex structures are classified as forerunners of the later palaces. In a number of sites, defensive walls were also erected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, new types of burials and more imposing ones have been unearthed, which display a great variety of luxurious objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield31_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield31-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Among the various burial types, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaft_grave" title="Shaft grave">shaft grave</a> became the most common form of elite burial, a feature that gave the name to the early period of Mycenaean Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield31_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield31-25">[25]</a></sup> Among the Mycenaean elite, deceased males were usually laid to rest in gold masks and funerary armor, and females in gold crowns and clothes gleaming with gold ornaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The royal shaft graves next to the acropolis of Mycenae, in particular the Grave Circles <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grave_Circle_A" title="Grave Circle A">A</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grave_Circle_B" title="Grave Circle B">B</a> signified the elevation of a native Greek-speaking royal dynasty whose economic power depended on long-distance sea trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this period, the Mycenaean centers witnessed increased contacts with the outside world and especially with the <a href="/wiki/Cyclades" title="Cyclades">Cyclades</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> centers in the island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield31_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield31-25">[25]</a></sup> Mycenaean presence appears to be also depicted in a fresco at <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_(Santorini)" title="Akrotiri (Santorini)">Akrotiri</a>, on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera" title="Thera">Thera</a> island, which possibly displays many warriors in <a href="/wiki/Boar%27s_tusk_helmet" title="Boar's tusk helmet">Boar's tusk helmets</a>, a feature typical of Mycenaean warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In the early 15th century, commerce intensified with Mycenaean pottery reaching the western coast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of the Shaft Grave era, a new and more imposing type of elite burial emerged, the <a href="/wiki/Beehive_tomb#Greece" title="Beehive tomb">Tholos</a>: large circular burial chambers with high vaulted roofs and a straight entry passage lined with stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Koine_era_.28c._1450_BC.E2.80.931250_BC.29">Koine era (c. 1450 BC–1250 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Koine era (c. 1450 BC–1250 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes,_fresco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2068" data-file-width="2700" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg/220px-La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg/330px-La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg/440px-La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes%2C_fresco.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Dame_de_Myc%C3%A8nes,_fresco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fresco depicting a female figure in the acropolis of Mycenae, 13th century BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Minoan_eruption" title="Minoan eruption">eruption of Thera</a>, which according to archaeological data occurred in c. 1500 BC, resulted in the decline of the Minoan civilization of Crete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This turn of events gave the opportunity to the Mycenaeans to spread their influence throughout the Aegean. Around c. 1450 BC, they were in control of Crete itself, including <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>, and colonized several other Aegean islands, reaching as far as <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield7172_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield7172-35">[35]</a></sup> Thus the Mycenaeans became the dominant power of the region, marking the beginning of the Mycenaean 'Koine' era (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Κοινή</span>, common), a highly uniform culture that spread in mainland Greece and the Aegean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the early 14th century BC, Mycenaean trade began to take advantage of the new trading opportunities in the Mediterranean after the Minoan collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield7172_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield7172-35">[35]</a></sup> The trade routes were expanded further reaching <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a> in the Near East, <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a> in Italy and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield7172_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield7172-35">[35]</a></sup> At that time (c. 1400 BC), the palace of Knossos yielded the earliest records of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> script, based on the previous <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">Linear A</a> of the Minoans. The use of the new script spread in mainland Greece and offers valuable insight of the administrative network of the palatial centers. However, the unearthed records are too fragmentary for a political reconstruction of Bronze Age Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder8_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder8-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:119px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_armour_1400BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="117" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Mycenaean_armour_1400BC.jpg" width="117"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_armour_1400BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mycenaean <a href="/wiki/Dendra_panoply" title="Dendra panoply">panoply</a>, found in Dendra, Argolid, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1400 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Excavations at Miletus, southwest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, indicate the existence of a Mycenaean settlement there already from c. 1450 BC, replacing the previous Minoan installations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This site became a sizable and prosperous Mycenaean center until the 12th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Apart from the archaeological evidence, this is also attested in Hittite records, which indicate that Miletos (Milawata in Hittite) was the most important base for Mycenaean activity in Asia Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryce_2005_361_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryce_2005_361-40">[40]</a></sup> Mycenaean presence also reached the adjacent sites of <a href="/wiki/Iasos" title="Iasos">Iasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, imposing palaces were built in the main Mycenaean centers of the mainland. The earliest palace structures were megaron-type buildings, such as the Menelaion in <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakonia" title="Lakonia">Lakonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder107_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder107-42">[42]</a></sup> Palaces proper are datable from c. 1400 BC, when <a href="/wiki/Cyclopean_masonry" title="Cyclopean masonry">Cyclopean fortifications</a> were erected at Mycenae and nearby <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields-2004-10_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-2004-10-1">[1]</a></sup> Additional palaces were built in <a href="/wiki/Midea_(Argolid)" title="Midea (Argolid)">Midea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleusis" title="Eleusis">Eleusis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Boeotia)" title="Orchomenus (Boeotia)">Orchomenos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Greece" title="Central Greece">Central Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iolcos" title="Iolcos">Iolcos</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>, the latter being the northernmost Mycenaean center. Knossos in Crete became also a Mycenaean center, where the former Minoan complex underwent a number of adjustments, including the addition of a <a href="/wiki/Throne_Room,_Knossos" title="Throne Room, Knossos">throne room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> These centers were based on a rigid network of bureaucracy where administrative competencies were classified into various sections and offices according to specialization of work and trades. At the head of this society was the king, known as <a href="/wiki/Anax" title="Anax">wanax</a> (Linear B: wa-na-ka) in Mycenaean Greek terms. All powers were vested in him, as the main landlord and spiritual and military leader. At the same time he was an entrepreneur and trader and was aided by a network of high officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_in_Asia_Minor">Involvement in Asia Minor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Involvement in Asia Minor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The presence of Ahhiyawa in western Anatolia is mentioned in various Hittite accounts from c. 1400 to c. 1220 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryce_2005_361_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bryce_2005_361-40">[40]</a></sup> Ahhiyawa is generally accepted as a Hittite translation of Mycenaean Greece (Achaeans in Homeric Greek), but a precise geographical definition of the term cannot be drawn from the texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckmanBryceCline6_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeckmanBryceCline6-45">[45]</a></sup> During this time, the kings of Ahhiyawa were evidently capable of dealing with their Hittite counterparts both on a diplomatic and military level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Moreover, Ahhiyawan activity was to interfere in Anatolian affairs, with the support of anti-Hittite uprisings or through local vassal rulers, which the Ahhiyawan king used as agents for the extension of his influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:14_century_BC_Eastern.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="1041" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/300px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/450px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/600px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:14_century_BC_Eastern.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eastern Mediterranean and the Middle East during the 14th century BC. Mycenaean Greece in purple.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In c. 1400 BC, Hittite records mention the military activities of an Ahhiyawan warlord, <a href="/wiki/Attarsiya" title="Attarsiya">Attarsiya</a>, a possible Hittite way of writing the Greek name <a href="/wiki/Atreus" title="Atreus">Atreus</a>, who attacked Hittite vassals in western Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Later, in c. 1315 BC, an anti-Hittite rebellion headed by <a href="/wiki/Arzawa" title="Arzawa">Arzawa</a>, a Hittite vassal state, received support from Ahhiyawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Ahhiyawa appears to be in control of a number of islands in the Aegean, an impression also supported by archaeological evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> During the reign of the Hittite king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hattusili_III" title="Hattusili III">Hattusili III</a> (c. 1267–1237 BC), the king of Ahhiyawa is recognized as a "Great King" and of equal status with the other contemporary great Bronze Age rulers: the kings of <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Babylonia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_period#Middle_Assyrian_Empire_1392-1056_BC" title="Middle Assyrian period">Assyria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> At that time, another anti-Hittite movement, led by <a href="/wiki/Piyama-Radu" title="Piyama-Radu">Piyama-Radu</a>, broke out and was supported by the king of Ahhiyawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Piyama-Radu caused major unrest in the region of <a href="/wiki/Wilusa" title="Wilusa">Wilusa</a> and later invaded the island of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a>, which then passed into Ahhiyawan control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder27_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder27-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hittite-Ahhiyawan confrontation in Wilusa, the Hittite name for <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>, may provide the <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_the_Iliad" title="Historicity of the Iliad">historical foundation</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> As a result of this instability, the Hittite king initiated <a href="/wiki/Tawagalawa_letter" title="Tawagalawa letter">correspondence</a> in order to convince his Ahhiyawan counterpart to restore peace in the region. The Hittite record mentions a certain Tawagalawa, a possible Hittite translation for Greek Eteocles, as brother of the king of Ahhiyawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder27_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder27-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_.28c._1250.E2.80.931100_BC.29">Collapse (c. 1250–1100 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Collapse (c. 1250–1100 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_decline_and_revival">Initial decline and revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Initial decline and revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="514" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg/220px-Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg/330px-Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg/440px-Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soldiers_Mycenae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marching soldiers observed by a female figure, in a <a href="/wiki/Warrior_Vase" title="Warrior Vase">krater</a> of c. 1200 BC in Mycenae.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1250 BC, the first wave of destruction apparently occurred in various centers of mainland Greece for reasons that cannot be identified by archaeologists. In <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a> was burned to the ground, around that year or slightly later. Nearby <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Boeotia)" title="Orchomenus (Boeotia)">Orchomenos</a> shared the same fate, while the Boeotian fortifications of <a href="/wiki/Gla" title="Gla">Gla</a> were deserted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder_2010_34_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder_2010_34-56">[56]</a></sup> In the Peloponnese, a number of buildings surrounding the citadel of Mycenae were attacked and burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline_2014_130_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline_2014_130-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>These incidents appear to have prompted the massive strengthening and expansion of the fortifications in various sites. In some cases, arrangements were also made for the creation of subterranean passages which led to underground cisterns. Tiryns, Midea and Athens expanded their defences with new cyclopean-style walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden219_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden219-58">[58]</a></sup> The extension program in Mycenae almost doubled the fortified area of the citadel. To this phase of extension belongs the impressive <a href="/wiki/Lion_Gate" title="Lion Gate">Lion Gate</a>, the main entrance into the Mycenaean acropolis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden219_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden219-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>It appears that after this first wave of destruction a short-lived revival of Mycenaean culture followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup> Mycenaean Greece continues to be mentioned in international affairs, particularly in Hittite records. In c. 1220 BC, the king of Ahhiyawa is again reported to have been involved in an anti-Hittite uprising in western Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder33_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder33-60">[60]</a></sup> Another contemporary Hittite account reports that Ahhiyawan ships should avoid <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a>-controlled harbors, as part of a trade embargo imposed on Assyria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In general, in the second half of 13th century BC, trade was in decline in the Eastern Mediterranean, most probably due to the unstable political environment there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_collapse">Final collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Final collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>None of the defence measures appear to have prevented the final destruction and collapse of the Mycenaean states. A second destruction struck Mycenae in ca. 1190 BC or shortly thereafter. This event marked the end of Mycenae as a major power. The site was then reoccupied, but on a smaller scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline_2014_130_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cline_2014_130-57">[57]</a></sup> The palace of <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>, in the southwestern Peloponnese, faced destruction in c. 1180 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tartaron18_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tartaron18-64">[64]</a></sup> The Linear B archives found there, preserved by the heat of the fire that destroyed the palace, mention hasty defence preparations due to an imminent attack without giving any detail about the attacking force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of this turmoil, specific regions in mainland Greece witnessed a dramatic population decrease, especially Boeotia, Argolis and <a href="/wiki/Messenia" title="Messenia">Messenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup> Mycenaean refugees migrated to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantine</a> coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tartaron18_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tartaron18-64">[64]</a></sup> Nevertheless, other regions on the edge of the Mycenaean world prospered, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_islands" title="Ionian islands">Ionian islands</a>, the northwestern Peloponnese, parts of <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a> and a number of Aegean islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">acropolis of Athens</a>, oddly, appears to have avoided destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses_for_the_collapse">Hypotheses for the collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hypotheses for the collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorian_Invasion" title="Dorian Invasion">Dorian Invasion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_Collapse.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="1132" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bronze_Age_Collapse.png/220px-Bronze_Age_Collapse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bronze_Age_Collapse.png/330px-Bronze_Age_Collapse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bronze_Age_Collapse.png/440px-Bronze_Age_Collapse.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_Collapse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Invasions, destructions and possible population movements during the collapse of the Bronze Age, ca. 1200 BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The reasons for the end of the Mycenaean culture have been hotly debated among scholars. At present, there is no satisfactory explanation for the collapse of the Mycenaean palace systems. The two most common theories are population movement and internal conflict. The first attributes the destruction of Mycenaean sites to invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hypothesis of a <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a>, known as such in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_Mythology" title="Ancient Greek Mythology">Ancient Greek tradition</a>, that led to the end of Mycenaean Greece, is supported by sporadic archaeological evidence such as new types of burials, in particular cist graves, and the use of a new dialect of Greek, the <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">Doric</a> one. It appears that the Dorians moved southward gradually over a number of years and devastated the territory, until they managed to establish themselves in the Mycenaean centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A new type of ceramic also appeared, called "Barbarian Ware" because it was attributed to invaders from the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-5"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup> On the other hand, the collapse of Mycenaean Greece coincides with the activity of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> in the Eastern Mediterranean. They caused widespread destruction in Anatolia and the Levant and were finally <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Delta" title="Battle of the Delta">defeated</a> by Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">Ramesses III</a> in c. 1175 BC. One of the ethnic groups that comprised these people were the <i>Eqwesh</i>, a name that appears to be linked with the Ahhiyawa of the Hittite inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alternative scenarios propose that the fall of Mycenaean Greece was a result of internal disturbances which led to internecine warfare among the Mycenaean states or civil unrest in a number of states, as a result of the strict hierarchical social system and the ideology of the wanax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tartaron19_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tartaron19-68">[68]</a></sup> In general, due to the obscure archaeological picture in 12th-11th century BC Greece, there is a continuing controversy among scholars over whether the impoverished societies that succeeded the Mycenaean palatial states were newcomers or populations that already resided in Mycenaean Greece. Recent archaeological findings tend to favor the latter scenario.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman126_59-6"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman126-59">[59]</a></sup> Additional theories, concerning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse#Nature_and_causes_of_destruction" title="Bronze Age collapse">natural factors</a>, such as climate change, droughts or earthquakes have also been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tartaron19_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tartaron19-68">[68]</a></sup> The period following the end of Mycenaean Greece, c. 1100-800 BC, is generally termed the "<a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_organization">Political organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="815" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg/320px-Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg/480px-Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg/640px-Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Palace_States.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reconstruction of the political landscape in c. 1400-1250 BC mainland southern Greece.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Palatial_states">Palatial states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Palatial states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mycenaean palatial states, or centrally organized palace-operating polities, are recorded in ancient <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">Greek literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">mythology</a> (e.g., <i>Iliad</i>, <a href="/wiki/Catalogue_of_Ships" title="Catalogue of Ships">Catalogue of Ships</a>) and confirmed by discoveries made by modern archaeologists such as <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a>. Each Mycenaean kingdom was governed from the palace, which exercised control over most, if not all, industries within its realm. The palatial territory was divided into several sub-regions, each headed by its provincial center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder9_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder9-70">[70]</a></sup> Each province was further divided in smaller districts, the <i>da-mo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder9_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder9-70">[70]</a></sup> A number of palaces and fortifications appear to be part of a wider kingdom. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Gla" title="Gla">Gla</a>, located in the region of Boeotia, belonged to the state of nearby Orchomenos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder_2010_34_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder_2010_34-56">[56]</a></sup> Moreover, the palace of Mycenae appeared to have ruled over a territory two to three times the size of the other palatial states in Bronze Age Greece. Its territory would have also included adjacent centers, including <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nafplio" title="Nafplio">Nauplion</a>, which could plausibly be ruled by a member of Mycenae's ruling dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The unearthed Linear B texts are too fragmentary for the reconstruction of the political landscape in Mycenaean Greece and they do not support the existence of a larger Mycenaean state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckmanBryceCline6_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-BeckmanBryceCline6-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> On the other hand, contemporary Hittite and Egyptian records suggest the presence of a single state under a "Great King".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Alternatively, based on archaeological data, some sort of confederation among a number of palatial states appears to be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckmanBryceCline6_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-BeckmanBryceCline6-45">[45]</a></sup> If some kind of united political entity existed, the dominant center was probably located in Thebes or in Mycenae, with the latter state being the most probable center of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_administration">Society and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Society and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Neolithic agrarian village (6000 BC) constituted the foundation of Bronze Age political culture in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The vast majority of the preserved Linear B records deal with administrative issues and give the impression that Mycenaean palatial administration was highly uniform with the use of the same language, terminology, system of taxation and distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder8_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder8-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder9_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder9-70">[70]</a></sup> Considering this sense of uniformity, the <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> archive, which is the best preserved one in the Mycenaean world, is generally taken as a representative one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder8_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder8-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state was ruled by a king, the <i><a href="/wiki/Anax" title="Anax">wanax</a></i> (ϝάναξ), whose role was religious and perhaps also military and judicial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-76">[76]</a></sup> The wanax oversaw virtually all aspects of palatial life, from religious feasting and offerings to the distribution of goods, craftsmen and troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder11_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder11-77">[77]</a></sup> Under him was the <i>lāwāgetas</i> ("the leader of the people"), whose role appears mainly religious. His activities possibly overlap with the wanax and is usually seen as the second-in-command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder11_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder11-77">[77]</a></sup> Both wanax and lāwāgetas were at the head of a military aristocracy known as the <i>eqeta</i> ("companions" or "followers").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The land possessed by the wanax is usually the <i>témenos</i> (te-me-no). There is also at least one instance of a person, <a href="/wiki/Enkhelyawon" title="Enkhelyawon">Enkhelyawon</a>, at Pylos, who appears titleless in the written record but whom modern scholars regard as probably a king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick71-72_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick71-72-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of local officials positioned by the wanax appear to be in charge of the districts, such as <i>ko-re-te</i> (<i>koreter</i>, '"governor"), <i>po-ro-ko-re-te</i> (<i>prokoreter</i>, "deputy") and the <i>da-mo-ko-ro</i> (<i>damokoros</i>, "one who takes care of a damos"), the latter probably being appointed to take charge of the commune. A council of elders was chaired, the <i>ke-ro-si-ja</i> (cf. γερουσία, <i>gerousía</i>). The <i>basileus</i>, who in latter Greek society was the name of the king, refers to communal officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, Mycenaean society appears to have been divided into two groups of free men: the king's entourage, who conducted administrative duties at the palace, and the people, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B4%E1%BF%86%CE%BC%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:δῆμος">da-mo</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> These last were watched over by royal agents and were obliged to perform duties for and pay taxes to the palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-76">[76]</a></sup> Among those who could be found in the palace were well-to-do high officials, who probably lived in the vast residences found in proximity to Mycenaean palaces, but also others, tied by their work to the palace and not necessarily better off than the members of the <i>da-mo</i>, such as craftsmen, farmers, and perhaps merchants. Occupying a lower rung of the social ladder were the slaves, <i>do-e-ro</i>, (<a href="/wiki/Cf." title="Cf.">cf.</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">δοῦλος</span>, <i>doúlos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> These are recorded in the texts as working either for the palace or for specific deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG/220px-Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG/330px-Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG/440px-Ancient_Greek_pottery_in_the_National_Archaeological_Museum_in_Athens_13.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
