</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mycenaean economy, given its pre-monetary nature, was focused on the redistribution of goods, commodities and labor by a central administration. The preserved Linear B records in Pylos and Knossos indicate that the palaces were closely monitoring a variety of industries and commodities, the organization of land management and the rations given to the dependent personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen7_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen7-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline145_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline145-83">[83]</a></sup> The Mycenaean palaces maintained extensive control of the nondomestic areas of production through careful control and acquisition and distribution in the palace industries, and the tallying of produced goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline242_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline242-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budin94_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budin94-85">[85]</a></sup> For instance, the Knossos tablets record c. 80,000-100,000 sheep grazing in central <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, and the quantity of the expected wool from these sheep and their offspring, as well as how this wool was allocated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budin94_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Budin94-85">[85]</a></sup> The archives of Pylos display a specialized workforce, where each worker belonged to a precise category and was assigned to a specific task in the stages of production, notably in textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, palatial control over resources appears to have been highly selective in spatial terms and in terms of how different industries were managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Thus, sectors like the production of perfumed oil and bronze materials were directly monitored from the palace, but the production of ceramics was only indirectly monitored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Regional transactions between the palaces are also recorded on a few occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large-scale_infrastructure">Large-scale infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Large-scale infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The palatial centers organized their workforce and resources for the construction of large scale projects in the fields of agriculture and industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline145_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cline145-83">[83]</a></sup> The magnitude of some projects indicates that this was the result of combined efforts from multiple palatial centers. Most notable of them are the drainage system of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Copais" title="Lake Copais">Kopais</a> basin in Boeotia, the building of a large dam outside Tiryns, and the drainage of the swamp in the <a href="/wiki/Nemea" title="Nemea">Nemea</a> valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder116_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder116-90">[90]</a></sup> Moreover, the construction of harbors, capable of accommodating large Bronze Age era vessels, such as the one found at <a href="/wiki/Uluburun_shipwreck" title="Uluburun shipwreck">Uluburun</a>, like in the case of the harbor of Pylos, is also noticeable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder116_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder116-90">[90]</a></sup> The Mycenaean economy also featured large-scale manufacturing as testified by the extent of workshop complexes that have been discovered, the largest known to date being the recent ceramic and hydraulic installations found in <a href="/wiki/Euonymeia" title="Euonymeia">Euonymeia</a>, next to Athens, that produced tableware, textiles, sails, and ropes for <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">export</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilstrap_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilstrap-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most famous project of the Mycenaean era was the network of roads in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder116_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder116-90">[90]</a></sup> This appears to have facilitated the speedy deployment of troops--for example, the remnants of a Mycenaean road, along with what appears to have been a Mycenaean defensive wall on the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a>. The Mycenaean era saw the zenith of infrastructure engineering in Greece, and this appears not to have been limited to the Argive plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2025" data-file-width="2278" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg/200px-Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg/300px-Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg/400px-Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earring_Mycenae_Louvre_Bj135.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gold earring, ca. 1600 BC, Louvre Museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Megiste-Bronze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Megiste-Bronze.jpg/220px-Megiste-Bronze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Megiste-Bronze.jpg/330px-Megiste-Bronze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Megiste-Bronze.jpg/440px-Megiste-Bronze.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Megiste-Bronze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reconstruction of a Mycenaean ship.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Trade over vast areas of the Mediterranean was essential for the economy of Mycenaean Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline145_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cline145-83">[83]</a></sup> The Mycenaean palaces imported raw materials, such as metals, ivory and glass, and exported processed commodities and objects made from these materials, in addition to local products: oil, perfume, wine, wool and pottery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline145_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cline145-83">[83]</a></sup> International trade of that time was not only conducted by palatial emissaries but also by independent merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline200_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline200-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Based on archaeological findings in the Middle East, in particular physical artifacts, textual references, inscriptions and wall paintings, it appears that Mycenaean Greeks achieved strong commercial and cultural interaction with most of the Bronze Age people living in this region: <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Assyrian_people#Ancient_Assyria" title="History of the Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptians" title="Ancient Egyptians">Egyptians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline200_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cline200-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The 14th century <a href="/wiki/Uluburun_shipwreck" title="Uluburun shipwreck">Uluburun shipwreck</a>, off the coast of southern Anatolia, displays the established trade routes that supplied the Mycenaeans with all the raw materials and items that the economy of Mycenaean Greece needed, such as copper and tin for the production of bronze products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> A chief export of the Mycenaeans was <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>, which was a multi-purpose product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cyprus appears to be the principal intermediary station between Mycenaean Greece and the Middle East, based on the considerable greater quantities of Mycenaean goods found there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> On the other hand, trade with the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> lands in central Anatolia appears to have been limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline200_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cline200-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Trade with <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> is also well attested, while Mycenaean trade routes expanded further to the <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a> and the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Mycenaean swords have been found as far away as <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> in the eastern Black Sea coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commercial interaction was also intense with the Italian peninsula and the western Mediterranean. Mycenaean products, especially pottery, were exported to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magna_Grecia" title="Magna Grecia">southern Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolian_islands" title="Aeolian islands">Aeolian islands</a>. Mycenaean products also penetrated further into <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> as well as southern <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sporadic objects of Mycenaean manufacture were found in various distant locations, like in Central Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> such as in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, where an amber object inscribed with Linear B symbols has been unearthed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Mycenaean bronze double axes and other objects dating from the 13th century BC have been found in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_religion" title="Mycenaean religion">Mycenaean religion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Mycenaean_gods" title="List of Mycenaean gods">List of Mycenaean gods</a></div>
<p>Temples and shrines are strangely rare in the Mycenaean archaeological sites. Monumental cultic structures are absent at all the palatial centres, with the exception of Mycenae. However, the cultic center of Mycenae seems to have been a later (13th century BC) development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder115_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder115-109">[109]</a></sup> Small shrines have been identified in <a href="/wiki/Asine" title="Asine">Asine</a>, Berbati, Malthi and Pylos,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> while a number of sacred enclosures have been located near Mycenae, <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amykles#Ancient_Amyclae" title="Amykles">Amyklae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Linear B records mention a number of sanctuaries dedicated to a variety of deities, at least in Pylos and Knossos. They also indicate that there were various religious festivities including offerings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Written Mycenaean records mention various priests and priestesses who were responsible for specific shrines and temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The latter were prominent figures in society, and the role of Mycenaean women in religious festivities was also important, just as in <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> Crete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Mycenaean pantheon already included many divinities that can be found in Classical Greece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> though it's difficult to claim whether these deities had the characteristics and responsibilities attributed to them at later periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden143_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden143-116">[116]</a></sup> In general, the same divinities were worshipped throughout the Mycenaean palatial world. There may be some indications for local deities at various sites, in particular in Crete. The uniformity of Mycenaean religion is also reflected in archaeological evidence with the phi- and psi-figurines that have been found all over Late Bronze Age Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder115_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder115-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> (Linear B: <i>Po-se-da-o</i>) seems to have occupied a place of privilege. He was a <a href="/wiki/Chthonic" title="Chthonic">chthonic</a> deity, connected with earthquakes (<i>E-ne-si-da-o-ne</i>: Earth-shaker), but it seems that he also represented the river spirit of the underworld.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paean_(god)" title="Paean (god)">Paean</a> (<i>Pa-ja-wo</i>) is probably the precursor of the Greek physician of the gods in Homer's <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>. He was the personification of the magic-song which was supposed to "heal" the patient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> A number of divinities have been identified in the Mycenaean scripts only by their <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a> used during later antiquity. For example, <i>Qo-wi-ja</i> ("cow-eyed") is a standard Homeric ephithet of Hera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_1976_95_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick_1976_95-119">[119]</a></sup> Ares appeared under the name <i>Enyalios</i> (assuming that <i>Enyalios</i> is not a separate god).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Additional divinities that can be also found in later periods include <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erinyes" title="Erinyes">Erinya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> (<i>a-te-mi-to</i> and <i>a-ti-mi-te</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dionysos" title="Dionysos">Dionysos</a> (<i>Di-wo-nu-so</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> also appears in the Mycenaean pantheon, but he was certainly not the chief deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden143_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden143-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>A collection of "ladies" or "mistresses", <i>Po-ti-ni-ja</i> (<i>Potnia</i>) are named in the Mycenaean scripts. As such, <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> (<i>A-ta-na</i>) appears in an inscription at <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> as <i>mistress Athena</i>, similar to a later <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric</a> expression, but in the Pylos tablets she is mentioned without any accompanying word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mylonas159_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mylonas159-125">[125]</a></sup> <i>Si-to po-ti-ni-ja</i> appears to be an agricultural goddess, possibly related to <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> of later antiquity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_1976_95_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick_1976_95-119">[119]</a></sup> while in Knossos there is the "mistress of the Labyrinth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The "two queens and the king" (<i>wa-na-ssoi</i>, <i>wa-na-ka-te</i>) are mentioned in Pylos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Goddess <i>Pe-re-swa</i> mentioned may be related to Persephone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chadwick_1976_95_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chadwick_1976_95-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mylonas159_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mylonas159-125">[125]</a></sup> A number of Mycenaean divinities seem to have no later equivalents, such as Marineus, Diwia and Komawenteia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden143_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden143-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Palaces">Palaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Palaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiryns.Castle.03.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="872" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tiryns.Castle.03.png/250px-Tiryns.Castle.03.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tiryns.Castle.03.png/375px-Tiryns.Castle.03.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tiryns.Castle.03.png/500px-Tiryns.Castle.03.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiryns.Castle.03.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a>, map of the palace and the surrounding fortifications.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The palatial structures at Mycenae, Tiryns and Pylos were erected on the summits of hills or rocky outcrops, dominating the immediate surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields19_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields19-129">[129]</a></sup> The best preserved are found in Pylos and Tiryns, while Mycenae and the Menelaion are only partially preserved. In Central Greece, Thebes and Orchomenos have been only partially exposed. On the other hand, the palace built at the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">acropolis of Athens</a> has been almost completely destroyed. A substantial building at <a href="/wiki/Dimini" title="Dimini">Dimini</a> in Thessaly, possibly ancient <a href="/wiki/Iolcos" title="Iolcos">Iolcos</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> is believed by a number of archaeologists to be a palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields19_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fields19-129">[129]</a></sup> A Mycenaean palace has been also unearthed in <a href="/wiki/Laconia" title="Laconia">Laconia</a>, near the modern village of Xirokambi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pilo001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2903" data-file-width="4085" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pilo001.jpg/220px-Pilo001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pilo001.jpg/330px-Pilo001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pilo001.jpg/440px-Pilo001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pilo001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The hearth of the <a href="/wiki/Megaron" title="Megaron">megaron</a> of Pylos.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The palatial structures of mainland Greece share a number of common features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup> The focal point of the socio-political aspect of a Mycenaean palace was the <a href="/wiki/Megaron" title="Megaron">megaron</a>, the throne room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields19_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fields19-129">[129]</a></sup> It was laid out around a circular hearth surrounded by four columns. The throne was generally found on the right-hand side upon entering the room, while the interior of the megaron was lavishly decorated, flaunting images designed intentionally to demonstrate the political and religious power of the ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Access to the megaron was provided through a court, which was reached from a <a href="/wiki/Propylaea" title="Propylaea">propylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup> The iconography of the palatial chambers is remarkably uniform throughout Greece. For instance, in Pylos and Tiryns the paintings are focused on marine motifs, providing depictions of octopi, fish and dolphins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Around the megaron a group of courtyards each opened upon several rooms of different dimensions, such as storerooms and workshops, as well as reception halls and living quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup> In general Mycenaean palaces have yielded a wealth of artifacts and fragmentary frescoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additional common features are shared by the palaces of Pylos, Mycenae and Tiryns;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup> a large court with colonnades lies directly in front of the central megaron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> while a second, but smaller, megaron is also found inside these structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup> The staircases in the palace of <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> indicate that the palaces had two stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The private quarters of the members of the royal family were presumably located on the second floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fortifications">Fortifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fortifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg/220px-MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg/330px-MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg/440px-MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MicenePortaLeoniMura.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cyclopean_masonry" title="Cyclopean masonry">Cyclopean masonry</a> in the southern walls of Mycenae</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The construction of defensive structures was closely linked to the establishment of the palaces in mainland Greece. The principal Mycenaean centers were well-fortified and usually situated on an elevated terrain, like on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">acropolis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a> or on coastal plains, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Gla" title="Gla">Gla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields10_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields10-138">[138]</a></sup> Mycenaean Greeks in general appreciated the symbolism of war as expressed in defensive architecture, reflected by the visual impressiveness of their fortifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields10_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fields10-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="498" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG/220px-Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG/330px-Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG/440px-Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiryns.Castle.02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part of the galleries within the walls of <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Cyclopean_masonry" title="Cyclopean masonry">Cyclopean</a></i> is the term normally applied to the masonry characteristics of Mycenaean fortification systems and describes walls built of large, unworked boulders more than 8 m (26 ft) thick and weighing several metric tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> They were roughly fitted together without the use of mortar or clay to bind them, though smaller hunks of limestone fill the interstices. Their placement formed a polygonal pattern giving the curtain wall an irregular but imposing appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields11_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields11-140">[140]</a></sup> At the top it would have been wide enough for a walkway with a narrow protective <a href="/wiki/Parapet" title="Parapet">parapet</a> on the outer edge and with hoop-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crenellations" title="Crenellations">crenellations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields11_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fields11-140">[140]</a></sup> The term <i>Cyclopean</i> was derived by the latter Greeks of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical era</a> who believed that only the mythical giants, the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a>, could have constructed such megalithic structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields10_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fields10-138">[138]</a></sup> On the other hand, cut stone masonry is used only in and around gateways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields11_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fields11-140">[140]</a></sup> Another typical feature of Mycenaean megalithic construction was the use of a relieving triangle above a lintel block--an opening, often triangular, designed to reduce the weight over the lintel. The space was filled with some lighter stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields11_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fields11-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cyclopean fortifications were typical of Mycenaean walls, especially at the citadels of Mycenae, Tiryns, <a href="/wiki/Argos#Ancient" title="Argos">Argos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrisso,_Phocis#History" title="Chrisso, Phocis">Crisa</a> and Athens, while smaller boulders are found in <a href="/wiki/Midea,_Greece" title="Midea, Greece">Midea</a> and large limestone slabs are found at Gla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields11_140-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fields11-140">[140]</a></sup> In the Mycenaean settlements found in <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, Cyclopean-style walls are also present,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> as well as in western Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Besides the citadels, isolated forts were also erected on various strategic locations. The fortification systems also incorporated technical refinements such as secret cisterns, galleries, sally ports and projecting bastions for the protection of gateways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields10_138-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fields10-138">[138]</a></sup> On the other hand, the palace of Pylos, although a major center of power, paradoxically appears to have been left without any defensive walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_architectural_features">Other architectural features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other architectural features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mycenaean domestic architecture originates mainly from earlier Middle Helladic traditions (c. 2000–1650 BC) both in shape, as well as in location of settlement. The observed uniformity in domestic architecture came probably as a result of a shared past among the communities of the Greek mainland rather than as a consequence of cultural expansion of the Mycenaean Koine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder107_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder107-42">[42]</a></sup> Moreover, varying sizes of mudbricks were used in the construction of buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelder109_132-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kelder109-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Contrary to popular belief, some Mycenaean representative buildings already featured roofs made of fired tiles, as in <a href="/wiki/Gla" title="Gla">Gla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Midea_(Argolid)" title="Midea (Argolid)">Midea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Mycenaean_Greece" title="Military of Mycenaean Greece">Military of Mycenaean Greece</a></div>
<p>The military nature of the Mycenaean Greeks is evident from the numerous weapons unearthed, the use of warrior and combat representations in contemporary art, and the preserved Greek <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline313_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline313-147">[147]</a></sup> The Mycenaeans invested in the development of military infrastructure, with military production and logistics being supervised directly from the palatial centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline313_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cline313-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> According to the Linear B records in the palace of <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>, every rural community (the <i>damos</i>) was obliged to supply a certain number of men who had to serve in the army. Similar service was also performed by the aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg/200px-Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg/300px-Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg/400px-Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Treasure.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replicas of Mycenaean swords and cups.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mycenaean armies were initially based on heavy infantry, equipped with spears, large shields and in some occasion armor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Later in the 13th century BC, Mycenaean warfare underwent major changes both in tactics and weaponry and armed units became more uniform and flexible, while weapons became smaller and lighter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline313_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cline313-147">[147]</a></sup> The spear remained the main weapon among Mycenaean warriors, while the sword played a secondary role in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard50_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard50-151">[151]</a></sup> Other offensive weapons used were bows, maces, axes, slings and javelins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard50_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howard50-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The precise role and contribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_chariot" title="War chariot">chariots</a> on the battlefield is a matter of dispute due to the lack of sufficient evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> It appears that chariots were initially used as fighting vehicles during the 16th to 14th centuries BC, while later, in the 13th century BC, their role was limited to battlefield transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Boar%27s_tusk_helmet" title="Boar's tusk helmet">boar's tusk helmet</a> was the most identifiable piece of Mycenaean armor in use from the beginning to the collapse of Mycenaean culture. It is also known from several depictions in contemporary art in Greece and the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salimbeti23_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salimbeti23-156">[156]</a></sup> A representative piece of Mycenaean armor is the <a href="/wiki/Dendra_panoply" title="Dendra panoply">Dendra panoply</a> (c. 1450–1400 BC) which consisted of a cuirass of a complete set of armor made up of several elements of bronze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In general, most features of the later <a href="/wiki/Hoplite#Equipment" title="Hoplite">hoplite panoply</a> of classical Greek antiquity, were already known to Mycenaean Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> "Figure-of-eight" shields were the most common type of Mycenaean shields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> During the Late Mycenaean period, smaller types of shields were adopted, either of completely circular shape, or almost circular with a part cut out from their lower edge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_craftwork">Art and craftwork</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Art and craftwork">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vessels">Vessels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Vessels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_pottery" title="Mycenaean pottery">Mycenaean pottery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG/200px-Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG/300px-Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG/400px-Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Exhibit_Archaeologycal_Museum_Athens.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver repoussé <a href="/wiki/Rhyton" title="Rhyton">rhyton</a> with gold horns, from <a href="/wiki/Grave_Circle_A,_Mycenae" title="Grave Circle A, Mycenae">Grave Circle A</a> at Mycenae, 16th century BC (Archaeological Museum, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Late Mycenaean period (1400–1200 BC), Mycenaean vessels/pottery exhibited similarities spanning a significant area of the Eastern Mediterranean (i.e., from the Levant to Sicily) and possibly reflecting a form of economic and political union centered at Mycenae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup> However, the pottery of Crete during this time remained distinct indicating a degree of autonomy on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup> The Mycenaean Greeks produced in large quantities a variety of diversely-styled vessels such as stirrup jars, large bowls, <a href="/wiki/Alabastron" title="Alabastron">alabastron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krater" title="Krater">krater</a> and stemmed cups (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kylix_(drinking_cup)" title="Kylix (drinking cup)">kylikes</a>) resembling champagne glasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stirrup jars (Linear B: <i>ka-ra-re-u</i>, khlareus; "oil vessel"), specifically, were first invented on the island of Crete during the 16th century BC and used widely by the Mycenaeans from 1400 BC onward for transporting and storing wine and oil; the jars were usually pear-shaped or globular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup> As for stemmed cups (or kylikes), they evolved from Ephyraean goblets and a large quantity was discovered at a site called the "Potter's Shop" located in Zygouries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-4"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup> Mycenaean drinking vessels such as the stemmed cups contained single decorative motifs such as a <a href="/wiki/Seashell" title="Seashell">shell</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flower</a> painted on the side facing away from the drinker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden135_161-5"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden135-161">[161]</a></sup> The Mycenaean Greeks also painted entire scenes (called "Pictorial Style") on their vessels depicting warriors, chariots, horses and deities reminiscent of events described in Homer's <i>Iliad</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Other items developed by the Mycenaeans include clay lamps,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> as well as metallic vessels such as bronze tripod cauldrons (or basins).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> A few examples of vessels in <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> are also known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Figures_and_figurines">Figures and figurines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Figures and figurines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Mycenaean period has not yielded sculpture of any great size. The statuary of the period consists for the most part of small terracotta figurines found at almost every Mycenaean site in mainland Greece--in tombs, in settlement debris, and occasionally in cult contexts (Tiryns, Agios Konstantinos on <a href="/wiki/Methana" title="Methana">Methana</a>). The majority of these figurines are female and anthropomorphic or zoomorphic. The female figurines can be subdivided into three groups which were popular at different periods: the earliest are the Phi-type, which look like the Greek letter phi and their arms give the upper body of the figurine a rounded shape. The Psi-type looks like the letter Greek psi: these have outstretched upraised arms. The latest (12th century BC) are the Tau-type: these figurines look like the Greek letter tau with folded(?) arms at right angles to the body. Most figurines wear a large 'polos' hat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> They are painted with stripes or zigzags in the same manner as the contemporary pottery and presumably made by the same potters. Their purpose is uncertain, but they may have served as both votive objects and toys: some are found in children's graves but the vast majority of fragments are from domestic rubbish deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The presence of many of these figurines on sites where worship took place in the Archaic and Classical periods (approximately 200 below the sanctuary of Athena at Delphi, others at the temple of <a href="/wiki/Aphaea" title="Aphaea">Aphaea</a> on Aegina, at the sanctuary of Apollo Maleatas above Epidauros and at Amyklae near Sparta), suggests both that many were indeed religious in nature, perhaps as votives, but also that later places of worship may well have first been used in the Mycenaean period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Larger male, female or bovine terracotta wheelmade figures are much rarer. An important group was found in the Temple at Mycenae together with coiled clay snakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> while others have been found at Tiryns and in the East and West Shrines at Phylakopi on the island of Melos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frescoes">Frescoes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Frescoes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Woman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="849" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mycenaean_Woman.jpg/150px-Mycenaean_Woman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mycenaean_Woman.jpg/225px-Mycenaean_Woman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Mycenaean_Woman.jpg/300px-Mycenaean_Woman.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mycenaean_Woman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fresco of a Mycenaean woman.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The painting of the Mycenaean age was much influenced by that of the Minoan age. Fragments of wall paintings have been found in or around the palaces (Pylos, Mycenae, Tiryns) and in domestic contexts (Zygouries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The largest complete wall painting depicting three female figures, probably goddesses, was found in the so-called "cult center" at Mycenae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Various themes are represented: hunting, <a href="/wiki/Bull-leaping" title="Bull-leaping">bull leaping</a> (tauromachy), battle scenes, processions, etc. Some scenes may be part of mythological narratives, but if so their meaning eludes us. Other frescoes include geometric or stylised motifs, also used on painted pottery (see above).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_practices">Burial practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Burial practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The usual form of burial during this period was inhumation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The earliest Mycenaean burials were mostly in individual graves in the form of a pit or a stone-lined <a href="/wiki/Cist" title="Cist">cist</a> and offerings were limited to pottery and occasional items of jewellery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Groups of pit or cist graves containing elite members of the community were sometimes covered by a tumulus (mound) in the manner established since the Middle Helladic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> It has been argued that this form dates back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurgan_culture" title="Kurgan culture">Kurgan culture</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> however, Mycenaean burials are in actuality an indigenous development of mainland Greece with the Shaft Graves housing native rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Pit and cist graves remained in use for single burials throughout the Mycenaean period alongside more elaborate family graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The shaft graves at Mycenae within Grave Circles <a href="/wiki/Grave_Circle_A,_Mycenae" title="Grave Circle A, Mycenae">A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grave_Circle_B,_Mycenae" title="Grave Circle B, Mycenae">B</a> belonging to the same period represent an alternative manner of grouping elite burials. Next to the deceased were found full sets of weapons, ornate staffs as well as gold and silver cups and other valuable objects which point to their social rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20090727_mykines32.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/20090727_mykines32.jpg/200px-20090727_mykines32.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/20090727_mykines32.jpg/300px-20090727_mykines32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/20090727_mykines32.jpg/400px-20090727_mykines32.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Treasury_Atreus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Treasury_Atreus.jpg/200px-Treasury_Atreus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Treasury_Atreus.jpg/300px-Treasury_Atreus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Treasury_Atreus.jpg/400px-Treasury_Atreus.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Treasury_of_Atreus" title="Treasury of Atreus">Treasury of Atreus</a>, 13th century BC royal tholos tomb near Mycenae: exterior (left) and interior (right) view.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning also in the Late Helladic period are to be seen communal tombs of rectangular form. Nevertheless, it is difficult to establish whether the different forms of burial represent a social hierarchization, as was formerly thought, with the <a href="/wiki/Beehive_tomb" title="Beehive tomb">"tholos"</a> being the tombs of the elite rulers, the individual tombs those of the leisure class, and the communal tombs those of the people. Cremations increased in number over the course of the period, becoming quite numerous in the last phase of the Mycenaean era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The tholos was introduced during the early 15th century as the new and more imposing form of elite burial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> The most impressive tombs of the Mycenaean era are the monumental royal tombs of Mycenae, undoubtedly intended for the royal family of the city. The most famous is the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_of_Atreus" title="Treasury of Atreus">Treasury of Atreus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Beehive_tomb" title="Beehive tomb">tholos</a>. A total of nine of such tholos tombs are found in the region of Mycenae, while six of them belong to a single period (Late Helladic IIa, c. 1400-1300 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> It has been argued that different dynasties or factions may have competed through conspicuous burial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="1836" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg/220px-NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg/330px-NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg/440px-NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Tablette_7671.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Linear B tablets (Mycenaean Greek).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In circa 1600 BC, the Mycenaean Greeks borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> its syllabic writing system (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">Linear A</a>) and developed their own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllabic_script" title="Syllabic script">syllabic script</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-184">[184]</a></sup> The Linear B script was utilized by the Mycenaean palaces in Greece for administrative purposes where economic transactions were recorded on clay tablets and some pottery in the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean dialect</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-184">[184]</a></sup> The Linear B tablets were first discovered in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> by English archaeologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Evans" title="Sir Arthur Evans">Sir Arthur Evans</a> c. 1900 and later deciphered by English architect and cryptographer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ventris" title="Michael Ventris">Michael Ventris</a> in 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Ventris's discovery of an archaic Greek dialect in the Linear B tablets demonstrated that Mycenaean Greek was "the oldest known Greek dialect, elements of which survived in Homer’s language as a result of a long oral tradition of epic poetry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 8th century BC, after the end of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, Greece emerged with a network of myths and legends, the greatest of all being that of the Trojan <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden2_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden2-187">[187]</a></sup> In general, the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> of classical antiquity idealized the Mycenaean period as a glorious period of heroes, closeness of the gods and material wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The legends of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s Epics were especially and generally accepted as part of the Greek past and it was not until the 19th century that scholars began to question Homer's historicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden2_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden2-187">[187]</a></sup> At this time, German archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a> undertook the first modern archaeological excavations in Greece at the site of <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a> in 1876.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Thus, Schliemann set out to prove the historical accuracy of the <i>Iliad</i> by identifying the places described by Homer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden2_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden2-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of the Mycenaean heritage that survived, the names of the gods and goddesses of Mycenaean Greece became major figures of the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Olympian Pantheon</a> of later antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Moreover, the language of the Mycenaeans offers the first written evidence of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> while a significant part of the Mycenaean vocabulary can also be found in modern English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Mycenaean Greeks were also pioneers in the field of <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, launching large-scale projects unmatched in Europe until the Roman period, such as fortifications, bridges, culverts, aqueducts, dams and roads suitable for wheeled traffic. They also made several architectural innovations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Discharging_arch#History" title="Discharging arch">relieving triangle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden231_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden231-193">[193]</a></sup> They were also responsible for transmitting a wide range of arts and crafts, especially of Minoan origin. The Mycenaean civilization was in general more advanced compared to the Late Bronze Age cultures of the rest of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Several Mycenaean attributes and achievements were borrowed or held in high regard in later periods; so, it would be no exaggeration to consider Mycenaean Greece as a cradle of civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castleden231_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden231-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mycenaean_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Museum_of_Chora" title="Archaeological Museum of Chora">Archaeological Museum of Chora</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_language" title="Mycenaean language">Mycenaean language</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCastleden2005">Castleden 2005</a>, p. 107: "Huge quantities of olive oil were produced and it must have been a major source of wealth. The simple fact that southern Greece is far more suitable climatically for olive production may explain why the Mycenaean civilization made far greater advances in the south than in the north. The oil had a variety of uses, in cooking, as a dressing, as soap, as lamp oil, and as a base for manufacturing unguents."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTandy2001">Tandy 2001</a>, p. 20: "In LH IBBB (ca. 1310-1190), Mycenaean material culture spread widely throughout coastal and inland Epirus; in this period Mycenaean engagement in Epirus was strongest, both quantitatively and qualitatively. Though the Kiperi tholos may have gone out of use early in LH IIIB, the Cyclopean wall found there, as well as those at Ephyra, Kastriza, and Ayia Eleni, cannot have been built (and probably after) LH IIIB."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFIacovou2013">Iacovou 2013</a>, p. 610. Iacovou quotes Vassos Karageorghis who states that "The introduction of 'Cyclopean'-type walls at the very beginning of the LC IIIA period at Enkomi, Kition, Sinda and Maa-Palaeokastron was due to the arrival of Mycenaean settlers in Cyprus."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Castleden135-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden135_161-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCastleden2005">Castleden 2005</a>, p. 135.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCastleden2005">Castleden 2005</a>, pp. 135–137: "Large kraters decorated in Pictorial Style are found almost exclusively in Cyprus, and for a long time it was naturally assumed that they were manufactured there, but a few examples have been found on the Greek mainland, mostly near Mycenae, and it has now been established that they were all manufactured at workshops close to Mycenae, probably at Berbati just to the east of the city, where there are the right clay sources. The ware was probably specifically made for export to Cyprus, where they were used as centerpieces for drinking ceremonies. The decoration appears to have been painted on at high speed and the effect is sometimes crude; Reynold Higgins calls it 'barbarous', which is a fair description, but the scenes showing warriors, horses and chariots can still tell us much about everyday life in Mycenaean Greece, and as much again about Mycenaean religious beliefs and mythology. One krater from Enkomi in Cyprus shows a charioteer with his groom riding along, perhaps into battle, while a long-robed god, Zeus perhaps, stands in his way holding the scales of destiny that will decide his fate. It is an archetypal scene reminiscent of several in the <i>Iliad</i>, where the gods are shown intervening in battle and deciding the outcome."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFurumark1941">Furumark 1941</a>, p. 78: "There are two types of Mycenaean lamps. One of these (type 321) has a broad horizontal lip with two opposite depressions for wicks. This type is the clay version of a Minoan stone lamp, known in many examples both from Crete and from the Mainland. The other (type 321 a) has one wick-spout and a handle at the opposite side."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Vallance, Richard (2015). <a class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/12633620" rel="nofollow">"Did you Know you Speak Mycenaean Greek? You do!"</a>. <i>academia.edu</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">30 March</span> 2016</span>. <q>Whether we realize it or not, not only are tens of thousands of English words direct derivatives of ancient Greek, but some are derived even from Mycenaean Greek, which makes them very ancient indeed!</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMycenaean+Greece&amp;rft.atitle=Did+you+Know+you+Speak+Mycenaean+Greek%3F+You+do%21&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft.aulast=Vallance&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.academia.edu%2F12633620&amp;rft.jtitle=academia.edu&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Castleden231-193"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden231_193-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castleden231_193-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCastleden2005">Castleden 2005</a>, p. 231</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-194"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-194">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCastleden2005">Castleden 2005</a>, p. 230.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col" style=";"><span style="float:left;width:6em"> </span>
<div id="SocietyCulture" style="font-size:114%">
<div class="hlist">
